<td colspan="2" class="noprint selfref" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Fonts" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Replacement_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td>

<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/Shalom" title="Shalom"><img alt="שָׁלוֹם" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Shalom2.svg/40px-Shalom2.svg.png" width="40" height="22" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Shalom2.svg/60px-Shalom2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Shalom2.svg/80px-Shalom2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="741" data-file-height="400" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Hebrew" title="Help:Hebrew">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of Hebrew letters.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Hebrew</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Shofar, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', Cardo, 'Noto Sans Hebrew', David, FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;" dir="rtl">עִבְרִית</span>‎, <i><span title="Hebrew transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Ivrit</span></i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">[ʔivˈʁit]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:He-Ivrit.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/He-Ivrit.ogg" class="internal" title="He-Ivrit.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">[ʕivˈɾit]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Ivrit1.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Ivrit1.ogg" class="internal" title="Ivrit1.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>) is a <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, spoken by over 9 million people worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-Behadrey-Haredim_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Behadrey-Haredim-8">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aboutworldlanguages.com_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutworldlanguages.com-5">[4]</a></sup> Historically, it is regarded as the language of the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> and their ancestors, although the language was not referred to by the name Hebrew in the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[note 2]</a></sup> The earliest examples of written <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Hebrew_alphabet" title="Paleo-Hebrew alphabet">Paleo-Hebrew</a> date from the 10th century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup> Hebrew belongs to the <a href="/wiki/West_Semitic_languages" title="West Semitic languages">West Semitic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> language family. Hebrew is the only living <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite</a> language left, and the only truly successful example of a revived <a href="/wiki/Dead_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Dead language">dead language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hebrew had ceased to be an everyday spoken language somewhere between 200 and 400 CE, declining since the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba revolt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ASB_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASB-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OxfordDictionaryChristianChurch_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordDictionaryChristianChurch-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-S.C3.A1enz-BadillosRH_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S.C3.A1enz-BadillosRH-16">[note 3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> were already in use as international languages, especially among elites and immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> It survived into the medieval period as the language of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_liturgy" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish liturgy">Jewish liturgy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">rabbinic literature</a>, intra-Jewish commerce, and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_literature#Poetry" title="Jewish literature">poetry</a>. Then, in the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language" title="Revival of the Hebrew language">it was revived</a> as a spoken and literary language. It became the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of Palestine's Jews, and subsequently of the State of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a>, in 1998, it was the language of 5 million people worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-e18_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e18-4">[3]</a></sup> After Israel, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> has the second largest Hebrew-speaking population, with 220,000 fluent speakers,<sup id="cite_ref-2009_survey_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2009_survey-18">[15]</a></sup> mostly from Israel.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew" title="Modern Hebrew">Modern Hebrew</a> is one of the two <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official languages</a> of the State of Israel (the other being <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a>), while premodern Hebrew is used for prayer or study in <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> communities around the world today. Ancient Hebrew is also the liturgical tongue of the <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">Samaritans</a>, while modern Hebrew or Arabic is their vernacular. As a foreign language, it is studied mostly by <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> and students of Judaism and Israel, and by <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_Israel" title="Archaeology of Israel">archaeologists</a> and linguists specializing in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and its civilizations, as well as by theologians in Christian seminaries.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> (the first five books), and most of the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, is written in <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a>, with much of its present form specifically in the dialect that scholars believe flourished around the 6th century BCE, around the time of the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">Babylonian captivity</a>. For this reason, Hebrew has been referred to by Jews as <i><span title="Hebrew transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><a href="/wiki/Leshon_Hakodesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Leshon Hakodesh">Leshon Hakodesh</a></span></i> (<span lang="he"><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Shofar, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', Cardo, 'Noto Sans Hebrew', David, FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;" dir="rtl">לשון הקדש</span>‎</span>), "the Holy Language", since ancient times.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrew_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The modern word "Hebrew" is derived from the word "Ivri" (plural "Ivrim"; English: <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">Hebrews</a>), one of several names for the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelite</a> (Jewish and Samaritan) people. It is traditionally understood to be an adjective based on the name of Abraham's ancestor, <a href="/wiki/Eber" title="Eber">Eber</a> ("Ever" <span lang="he">עבר</span> in Hebrew), mentioned in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Genesis+10%3A21&amp;version=NRSV">Genesis 10:21</a></span>. This name is possibly based upon the root "ʕ-b-r" (<span lang="he">עבר</span>) meaning "to cross over". Interpretations of the term "ʕibrim" link it to this verb; cross over and <a href="/wiki/Homiletics" title="Homiletics">homiletical</a> or the people who crossed over the river <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Bible, the Hebrew language is called <i><span title="Hebrew transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Yәhudit</span></i> (<span lang="he">יהודית</span>) because <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a> (<i><span title="Hebrew transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Yәhuda</span></i>) was the surviving kingdom at the time of the quotation (late 8th century BCE (Is 36, 2 Kings 18)). In <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Isaiah+19%3A18&amp;version=NRSV">Isaiah 19:18</a></span> it is called the "Language of Canaan" (<span lang="he">שפת כנען)</span>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrew_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hebrew belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite group of languages</a>. In turn, the Canaanite languages are a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic</a> family of languages.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Avraham Ben-Yosef, Hebrew flourished as a spoken language in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Kingdoms of Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a> during about 1200 to 586 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> Scholars debate the degree to which Hebrew was a spoken vernacular in ancient times following the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_exile" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonian exile">Babylonian exile</a>, when the predominant international language in the region was <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Aramaic">Old Aramaic</a>.</p>
<p>Hebrew was nearly extinct as a spoken language by <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, but it continued to be used as a literary language and as the liturgical language of Judaism, evolving various dialects of literary <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Hebrew" title="Medieval Hebrew">Medieval Hebrew</a>, until its <a href="/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language" title="Revival of the Hebrew language">revival as a spoken language</a> in the late 19th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oldest_Hebrew_inscriptions">Oldest Hebrew inscriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrew_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Oldest Hebrew inscriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Hebrew_writings" title="Ancient Hebrew writings">Ancient Hebrew writings</a></div>
<p>In July 2008 Israeli archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Yosef_Garfinkel" title="Yosef Garfinkel">Yossi Garfinkel</a> discovered a ceramic shard at <a href="/wiki/Khirbet_Qeiyafa" title="Khirbet Qeiyafa">Khirbet Qeiyafa</a> which he claimed may be the earliest Hebrew writing yet discovered, dating around 3000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> Hebrew University archaeologist Amihai Mazar said that the inscription was "proto-Canaanite" but cautioned that, "The differentiation between the scripts, and between the languages themselves in that period, remains unclear," and suggested that calling the text Hebrew might be going too far.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gezer_calendar" title="Gezer calendar">Gezer calendar</a> also dates back to the 10th century BCE at the beginning of the Monarchic Period, the traditional time of the reign of <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a>. Classified as Archaic Biblical Hebrew, the calendar presents a list of seasons and related agricultural activities. The <a href="/wiki/Gezer" title="Gezer">Gezer</a> calendar (named after the city in whose proximity it was found) is written in an old Semitic script, akin to the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician</a> one that through the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscans</a> later became the <a href="/wiki/Roman_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman script">Roman script</a>. The Gezer calendar is written without any vowels, and it does not use <a href="/wiki/Mater_lectionis" title="Mater lectionis">consonants to imply vowels</a> even in the places where later Hebrew spelling requires it.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silwan-inscr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silwan-inscr.jpg/220px-Silwan-inscr.jpg" width="220" height="66" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silwan-inscr.jpg/330px-Silwan-inscr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silwan-inscr.jpg/440px-Silwan-inscr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="681" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silwan-inscr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Shebna_Inscription" class="mw-redirect" title="Shebna Inscription">Shebna Inscription</a>, from the tomb of a royal steward found in <a href="/wiki/Siloam" title="Siloam">Siloam</a>, dates to the 7th century BCE.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Numerous older tablets have been found in the region with similar scripts written in other Semitic languages, for example <a href="/wiki/Protosinaitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Protosinaitic">Protosinaitic</a>. It is believed that the original shapes of the script go back to <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a>, though the phonetic values are instead inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Acrophonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Acrophonic">acrophonic</a> principle. The common ancestor of Hebrew and Phoenician is called <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite</a>, and was the first to use a Semitic alphabet distinct from Egyptian. One ancient document is the famous <a href="/wiki/Moabite_Stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Moabite Stone">Moabite Stone</a> written in the Moabite dialect; the <a href="/wiki/Siloam_Inscription" class="mw-redirect" title="Siloam Inscription">Siloam Inscription</a>, found near <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, is an early example of Hebrew. Less ancient samples of Archaic Hebrew include the <a href="/wiki/Ostracon" title="Ostracon">ostraca</a> found near <a href="/wiki/Lachish" class="mw-redirect" title="Lachish">Lachish</a> which describe events preceding the final capture of Jerusalem by <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_II_of_Babylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Nebuchadnezzar II of Babylon">Nebuchadnezzar</a> and the Babylonian captivity of 586 BCE.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Hebrew">Classical Hebrew</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrew_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classical Hebrew">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrew_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Biblical Hebrew">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a></div>
<p>In its widest sense, Biblical Hebrew means the spoken language of ancient Israel flourishing between the 10th century BCE and the turn of the 4th century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dvbrbw_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dvbrbw-25">[22]</a></sup> It comprises several evolving and overlapping dialects. The phases of Classical Hebrew are often named after important literary works associated with them.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Archaic_Biblical_Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaic Biblical Hebrew">Archaic Biblical Hebrew</a> from the 10th to the 6th century BCE, corresponding to the Monarchic Period until the Babylonian Exile and represented by certain texts in the Hebrew Bible (<a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>), notably the Song of Moses (Exodus 15) and the Song of Deborah (Judges 5). Also called Old Hebrew or Paleo-Hebrew. It was written in the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Hebrew_alphabet" title="Paleo-Hebrew alphabet">Paleo-Hebrew alphabet</a>. A script descended from this, the <a href="/wiki/Samaritan_alphabet" title="Samaritan alphabet">Samaritan alphabet</a>, is still used by the <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">Samaritans</a>.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg/220px-Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg" width="220" height="70" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg/330px-Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg/440px-Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="448" data-file-height="142" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sefer-torah-vayehi-binsoa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hebrew script used in writing a Torah scroll. Note ornamental "crowns" on tops of certain letters.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Standard Biblical Hebrew</a> around the 8th to 6th centuries BCE, corresponding to the late Monarchic period and the Babylonian Exile. It is represented by the bulk of the Hebrew Bible that attains much of its present form around this time. Also called Biblical Hebrew, Early Biblical Hebrew, Classical Biblical Hebrew (or Classical Hebrew in the narrowest sense).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Late_Biblical_Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Biblical Hebrew">Late Biblical Hebrew</a>, from the 5th to the 3rd centuries BCE, that corresponds to the Persian Period and is represented by certain texts in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, notably the books of Ezra and Nehemiah. Basically similar to Classical Biblical Hebrew, apart from a few foreign words adopted for mainly governmental terms, and some syntactical innovations such as the use of the particle <i>shel</i> (of, belonging to). It adopted the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Imperial Aramaic script</a> (from which the modern Hebrew script descends).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Israelian_Hebrew" title="Israelian Hebrew">Israelian Hebrew</a> is a proposed northern dialect of biblical Hebrew, attested in all eras of the language, in some cases competing with late biblical Hebrew as an explanation for non-standard linguistic features of biblical texts.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_post-Biblical_Hebrew">Early post-Biblical Hebrew</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrew_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early post-Biblical Hebrew">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls" title="Dead Sea Scrolls">Dead Sea Scroll</a> Hebrew from the 3rd century BCE to the 1st century CE, corresponding to the Hellenistic and Roman Periods before the destruction of the Temple in Jerusalem and represented by the Qumran Scrolls that form most (but not all) of the Dead Sea Scrolls. Commonly abbreviated as DSS Hebrew, also called Qumran Hebrew. The Imperial Aramaic script of the earlier scrolls in the 3rd century BCE evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_square_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew square script">Hebrew square script</a> of the later scrolls in the 1st century CE, also known as <i>ketav Ashuri</i> (Assyrian script), still in use today.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mishnaic_Hebrew" title="Mishnaic Hebrew">Mishnaic Hebrew</a> from the 1st to the 3rd or 4th century CE, corresponding to the Roman Period after the destruction of the Temple in Jerusalem and represented by the bulk of the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tosefta" title="Tosefta">Tosefta</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> and by the Dead Sea Scrolls, notably the <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba</a> letters and the <a href="/wiki/Copper_Scroll" title="Copper Scroll">Copper Scroll</a>. Also called Tannaitic Hebrew or Early Rabbinic Hebrew.</li>
</ul>
<p>Sometimes the above phases of spoken Classical Hebrew are simplified into "Biblical Hebrew" (including several dialects from the 10th century BCE to 2nd century BCE and extant in certain Dead Sea Scrolls) and "Mishnaic Hebrew" (including several dialects from the 3rd century BCE to the 3rd century CE and extant in certain other Dead Sea Scrolls).<sup id="cite_ref-Segal_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Segal-26">[23]</a></sup> However, today, most Hebrew linguists classify Dead Sea Scroll Hebrew as a set of dialects evolving out of Late Biblical Hebrew and into Mishnaic Hebrew, thus including elements from both but remaining distinct from either.<sup id="cite_ref-Qimron_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qimron-27">[24]</a></sup> By the start of the Byzantine Period in the 4th century CE, Classical Hebrew ceases as a regularly spoken language, roughly a century after the publication of the Mishnah, apparently declining since the aftermath of the catastrophic <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba War</a> around 135 CE.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Displacement_by_Aramaic">Displacement by Aramaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hebrew_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Displacement by Aramaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rashiscript.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG/220px-Rashiscript.PNG" width="220" height="56" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG/330px-Rashiscript.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG 2x" data-file-width="341" data-file-height="87" /></a>
